#### Solution: rotate an array
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's create an algorithm that rotates the contents of an array to the left by one.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=6)** We start off with an input array.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=8)** After the rotation, the new array should contain 2 through 6 and then 1.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=15)** Let's implement it.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=18)** The first step is to create a new array with the same size as the input array.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=26)** This will create a new array, the same size as numbers, but with each item set to 0.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=34)** Next, we'll copy the elements from the numbers array to the rotated array, but at one index to the left.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=44)** This means we'll want to set the item at index 0 of the rotated array to be the item at index 1 of the numbers array.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=54)** We'll also want to set the item at index 1 of the rotated array to be the item at index 2 of the numbers array.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=64)** And this pattern continues on with the next set until the end of the array.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=70)** Seeing this pattern, we can set the item at index i-1 of the rotated array to be the item at index i of the numbers array.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=82)** This shifts all the contents to the left and it'll work for most of our array elements.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=88)** Unfortunately, we will run into an issue with the first item of the rotated array.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=95)** If we followed the same pattern, we'd get an index out-of-bounds exception, since the index -1 is invalid.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=104)** We can accommodate this by setting the last item in the rotated array to be the first item in the numbers array.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=116)** Now all we have to do is modify our loop so it only iterates through 1 to the length of the array.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=125)** That's our algorithm.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=128)** Let's try it.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-rotate-an-array-19866903?u=76281980&t=131)** The output is as we expect, we get 2 through 6 and then 1.

### 3. Linked List Algorithms

#### What is a linked list?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Linked list is another common data structure that you can use to store and retrieve useful data.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=7)** Similar to an array, t's a linear data structure where elements are ordered one after the other.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=14)** However, with a linked list, elements are not stored at contiguous locations.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=21)** Instead they're linked to using pointers.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=24)** The idea of an index does not exist for linked lists.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=29)** We call each element in a linked list, a node.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=33)** Each node contains a piece of data and a pointer to the location of the next piece of data.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=40)** Several nodes can be linked together through these next pointers.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=45)** In this example, the node containing five is the first node.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=50)** Through its next pointer, it's linked to the node eight, making the node containing eight the second element in the link to list.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=59)** The last element, 13 is linked through the eight nodes next pointer.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=65)** This last element of the list also contains the next pointer, but it points to null because there is no next element in the list.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=73)** It's the end of the list.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=76)** The first node in the list is often referred to as the head of the list.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=81)** And the last node is called the tail.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=84)** Now, since the data is not stored contiguously, the size of a linked list can vary over time.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=91)** With an array, it's size must be set as soon as it's created and the size can not change.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=98)** In order to store more elements in a given array, a bigger array must be created and the current items can be copied over to this larger array, along with the new items.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=111)** With the linked list, this is not needed because elements are linked by pointers, which can be changed or removed at any time.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=120)** For example, if you need to delete half of the data set, you can simply find the middle pointer and set it to null.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=128)** This would unlink the back half of the dataset, removing it from the list.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=134)** In other languages, there might be more memory cleanup required, but in C# we have a garbage collector and this memory manager will handle it for us.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=145)** Operations, like these were major data modifications can be made with just a few lines of code make link lists a useful tool when creating algorithms that manipulate your data.

>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=198)** Previously, we talked about how linked lists are built on the idea of nodes, but there are no nodes here.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=206)** The idea of nodes in the next pointer are actually abstracted from us through this built in linked list class.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=214)** If we click into the linked list class, I'm using command click on Mac.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=219)** We can see more details about how this structure works in C#.
>

>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=243)** To create a node, we give it a value.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=247)** We can also retrieve its next pointer and the value that the node holds.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=254)** Understanding the implementation of C# built-in data structures can help you make better choices about what tools you use in your algorithms, which is why we've explored it here.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=265)** Since the state of structure is built in, we don't have to worry about the maintenance of modifying next pointers or the implementation of a list every time we add data.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=277)** It provides an interface, so you can just worry about the data you want to add and when, rather than the intricacies of how that data is stored and retrieved in the list.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=289)** Simplifying complex operations through interfaces is a great principle to follow when creating data structures and developing algorithms.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/common-linked-list-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=300)** It's also what makes the linked list a great addition to your software development toolkit.

>

>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=180)** Let's start with iterating through the list.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=183)** Since this custom linked list is not a part of the collections interface, we'll need to iterate through it using next pointers.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=193)** With every type of loop, we need a variable that keeps track of where we are in the loop.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=199)** Let's create a current variable that keeps track of what node we're looking at.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=204)** We'll start it at the head node.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=206)** Then, while the current node is not equal to null, we'll iterate through the list.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=213)** To start off, we'll just print the node's data, Console.Write, current.data.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=222)** Then we'll increment current by setting current equal to current.next.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=228)** This puts us at the next element in the list.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=233)** It's important to check whether the current element is null before accessing the data or next attributes.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=241)** If a given node is null, and we try to access its attributes, we'll get a null pointer exception, because you can't access an attribute of null.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=251)** Let's try running the function as it is now.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=256)** We'll access the linked list and call deleteBackHalf.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=262)** It won't delete any data yet, because we're only iterating through the list.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=267)** However, we will be able to see the contents in the console.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=274)** And there's our data.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=276)** Now, this functionality is actually pretty useful for displaying the data in our linked list.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=282)** So let's copy the implementation and put it in a new function called displayContents.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=292)** Back to our deleteBackHalf function, we need a way to determine whether we're in the middle of the list.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=299)** One common strategy is to use two pointers, a slow pointer and a fast pointer.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=305)** Instead of having current as our tracker for where we are in the list, we'll have two trackers, slow and fast.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=313)** Both of these will start at the head, but the fast pointer will move twice as fast as the slow pointer.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=321)** When the fast pointer reaches the end of the list, the slow pointer will be at the middle element of the list.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=330)** This means, on the iteration, we'll have slow = slow.next; and fast = fast.next.next;
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=346)** Now, since we're accessing fast.next and fast.next.next, we'll need to make sure these variables are not null before entering the while loop.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=357)** We don't need to check whether the slow node is null, because it will be behind the fast node.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=365)** If either of these conditions become false, the fast pointer is either at the end of the list, or at the last element of the list.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=375)** This puts our slow pointer at the middle node.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=379)** In order to delete the back half, we'll want to access the node just before the slow node, so we can set its next pointer equal to null.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=389)** This will delete the middle node, as well as everything after it.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=394)** We can do this using a previous variable.
>
> **[6:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=399)** The previous node will run just behind the slow node.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=404)** At the end of the while loop's execution, all we have to do is set the previous node's next reference to null.
>
> **[6:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=415)** This deletes the middle node, and everything after it.
>
> **[6:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=419)** We're almost done.
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=421)** We just need to set up our base cases.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=424)** If the while loop condition is true the first time it's executed, then previous will still be null, and we'll get an error because we're setting an attribute of a null object.
>
> **[7:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=437)** To fix this, we can check whether the head is null or the head.next is null, before entering the while loop.
>
> **[7:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=447)** In both of these cases, the list must have zero elements or only one element.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=454)** Regardless, we'll want to set the head equal to null.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=460)** This will keep the list empty, or it will delete the single item in the list.
>
> **[7:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=466)** Let's try out our function.
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=474)** We'll display the contents of the list, delete the back half, and then display the contents again.
>
> **[8:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=484)** We'll also write a new line, so they're displayed on separate lines.
>
> **[8:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=489)** Let's run it.
>
> **[8:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=492)** The output is as expected.
>
> **[8:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/linked-list-algorithms?u=76281980&t=494)** By using different variables and creating custom data structures, we can develop algorithms that let us manipulate our data in exactly the way we want.

#### Solution: Sum contents of a linked list
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's implement the sum function.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=3)** We'll start off by creating a variable called sum to keep track of the sum of our items in the linked list.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=11)** We'll also want a variable for keeping track of where we are in the list.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=16)** We'll create a variable called current and set it to the beginning of the list.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=22)** With our variables set up, now it's time to iterate through the list, adding each item's value to the sum.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=30)** We can do this with a while loop.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=34)** While the current item in the linked list is not equal to null, we'll want to retrieve its data, add it to the sum, and go to the next item in the list.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=45)** We can access an item's data with the val field.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=50)** Then we'll add it to the list.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=53)** Let's increment the current variable.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=57)** We'll set current = current.next, moving us along in the list.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=64)** Once we've iterated through the list, we can return the sum.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=69)** That's our implementation.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=72)** Let's run it.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=75)** In the console, we get 20.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/solution-sum-contents-of-a-linked-list?u=76281980&t=79)** That's the result of 7 + 5 + 3 + 4 + 1 Our solution works as expected.

### 4. Queue and Stack Algorithms

#### What is a queue?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The queue is another data structure that can be particularly useful in algorithms where you need to process data in order.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=9)** Like an array or list, a queue represents a series of objects, but the way we access, add, and remove items is slightly different.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=20)** A queue contains the elements in the order they were added.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=24)** It's designed to have elements inserted at the end of the queue, and elements removed from the beginning of the queue.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=32)** You cannot remove elements from the middle or end.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=36)** You must remove items from the front first.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=39)** Similarly, you cannot add items to the middle or front.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=44)** You must add items to the back.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=47)** For this reason, we say queues, follow a FIFO, or first in, first out policy.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=54)** The first item in the queue must be the first item out of the queue.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=59)** Now, when we talk about adding and removing items, we refer to it as enqueuing and dequeuing.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=67)** When we add an item, we enqueue it to the back of the queue.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=71)** When we remove an item, we dequeue it from the front of the queue.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=76)** We can not add an element to a certain index, because indices do not exist for queues.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=83)** There's simply enqueuing to the back and dequeuing from the front.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=88)** As for accessing items, we can only access the item at the front of the queue.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=93)** If we want to access an item towards the back of the queue, we'll need to remove each item in front of it.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=102)** While this FIFO policy might seem like a restriction, it actually becomes quite useful in algorithms because we're adding on a guaranteed assumption.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-queue?u=76281980&t=112)** We can leverage the queue's first in, first out policy to make our algorithms more efficient.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** fifo (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In C-sharp, we can use the built-in queue data structure in the System.Collections.Generic namespace.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=10)** To create a queue, we can use the queue constructor.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=14)** This queue will contain integers.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=19)** Just like the built-in linked list class, the queue class is generic, so it can contain elements of any type you specify.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=28)** To add elements to the queue, we can use the enqueue method.

> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=34)** We'll enqueue one, eight and a few other numbers.

> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=46)** Now to follow the FI/FO policy, the first item in the queue must be the first item out of the queue.

>

>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=155)** If the queue is empty and it cannot dequeue the item, it will return false.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=161)** It's important to use this method because if we try to dequeue or even peek at a queue that is empty, we'll get an invalid operation exception.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=172)** Now since TryDequeue will actually dequeue the item, we'll need to pass in a variable to save the dequeued item.

>

>

>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=200)** In the body of the while loop, we'll have access to the most recently removed item through this current variable.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=208)** Let's display it with a printout.

>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=246)** If you need to process data in the order it's received, the queue data structure is a great tool to add to your algorithms.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/standard-queue-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=255)** Queues reduce complexity by removing the index and applying the constraint of first in, first out, making your data easy to work with.

#### Queue algorithms: Generate binary numbers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] While the C# standard library has a built-in queue data structure with a few basic operations, you need to know how to leverage this functionality in order to work with your data efficiently.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=13)** Let's say we want to create an algorithm that takes in a number N as input.

> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=25)** A binary number is a number that consists of ones and zeros, and is on the base-2 numeral system.

>

>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=50)** The algorithm we create should generate as many of these numbers as we specify.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=56)** For example, if the input was 5, the algorithm would print out one, one zero, one one, one zero zero, and one O one.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=66)** If the input is less than or equal to 0, the algorithm shouldn't do anything; nothing should be printed out.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=74)** Now you might notice a pattern to these binary numbers.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=78)** We print an item, in this case, 1, and then print that same item with a 0 appended to it.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=85)** Then we print that item with a 1 appended to it.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=89)** After the items associated with the first number have been printed, we print the next item with a 0 appended to it, and then a 1 appended to it.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=99)** We process the first item, and then the second item, in a FIFO fashion.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=104)** This pattern follows throughout the rest of the binary numbers.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=108)** We can leverage this pattern with the queue data structure to create an efficient algorithm that generates binary numbers.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=116)** Let's try it.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=118)** We'll create a function called print binary.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=123)** It will take in the number of binary numbers we should print out.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=128)** In the implementation, we first need to check that the input is valid.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=133)** If the input is valid, N is greater than 0.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=138)** So if N is less than or equal to 0, we'll just return.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=145)** Now if the input is valid, we'll create a queue that will help us generate the next item to be printed.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=153)** It will hold integers.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=157)** We could use strings instead, but integers will turn out to be more efficient, due to the fact that strings are immutable.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=165)** The first binary number is 1, so we'll enqueue one to the queue.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=171)** Then we'll get into the iteration, where we can start displaying the binary numbers.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=178)** We'll want to display N numbers, so we'll go up to N for the iteration.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=183)** In the binary number pattern, we print an item, and then print the item with a 0, and then a 1.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=190)** We don't want to print all three numbers at once, because the number of items we want to print might be less than three.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=198)** We want to print exactly N numbers.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=202)** Instead of printing them now, we can save these numbers in the queue.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=207)** So to start, we'll take the item off the top of the queue and display it.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=215)** We'll dequeue it and write it to the console.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=221)** Then we'll save the two items that should follow later in the queue.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=226)** We'll enqueue the current item with a 0 appended to it.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=231)** To append a 0, we can multiply current by 10.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=237)** Next, we'll enqueue current times 10 plus 1.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=243)** This will append the current number with a 1 appended to it.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=247)** That follows the pattern.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=251)** Once we've printed out all of the binary numbers, we'll print out a new line.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=258)** And that's the end of the implementation.

> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/queue-algorithms-generate-binary-numbers?u=76281980&t=313)** Whenever there's a first in, first out pattern with your data, queues can be a very useful tool in your algorithm tool kit.

#### What is a stack?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The stack data structure can be useful for algorithms where you need to keep track of state.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=6)** A stack is a series of ordered objects, just like a list, but similar to a queue, the way we add access and remove items is a little different.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=17)** When we work with the stack, we push objects on the top of the stack and pop objects off the stack.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=24)** Items are added and removed from the same end.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=28)** We add to the top and remove from the top.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=32)** Stacks follow a last in, first out, policy or a LIFO policy.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=38)** If we add and remove from the same location, the last item pushed onto the stack will be the first item removed from the stack.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=47)** Another way to think about this is that the first item pushed onto the stack will be the last item popped off.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=55)** We can push as many items as we want onto the stack, but in order to retrieve a specific item, we must pop off each item in front of it to get it.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=67)** Once all the items have been popped off the stack, the stack is empty.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=72)** One example of a stack is the runtime stack, which keeps track of the execution of the program and processing nested functions.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=82)** First, the main function is called.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=85)** Then a builder function might be used.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=89)** Then a util function might be called.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=91)** The runtime stack also stores information about each sub-routine as they're used.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=99)** Whenever you get an error from the runtime stack, the error messages help you retrace your steps from where the error occurred.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/what-is-a-stack?u=76281980&t=107)** It keeps track of the state of your program, and you can create your own algorithms that leverage the stack data structure as well.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] C# has a built-in stack data structure that we can use in our custom algorithms.

> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=7)** It lives in the System.Collections.Generic namespace.

>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=95)** The stack helps us keep track of where each function was called.

>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=171)** Then the response builder is called.

>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=182)** Once that finishes, the response is returned through the response builder to the main function.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=189)** This ends the execution of the program.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=192)** If an error ever popped up in our program, the stack would help us find exactly where that error occurred since it's keeping track of the state of our program.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=203)** In addition to these two methods, push and pop, we also have a few others, stack.Peek and stack.TryPeek.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=214)** These are similar to the queue data structure where we'll peek on top of the stack without removing the item.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=223)** TryPeek in particular can help us find out if the stack is empty without raising an error since peeking at an empty stack raises an exception.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=234)** Since the stack is empty, TryPeek should return false because there's no item to peek at.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=243)** Running the program, we get false in the console.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=246)** If you need to keep track of state in your algorithm, the stack data structure is a great tool.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/basic-stack-operations-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=253)** Like queues, they reduce complexity by removing the index and applying the constraint of last in first out, making your data easy to work with.

#### Stack algorithms: Theorizing an algorithm
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As a software engineer, you'll need to know how to leverage the built-in stack functionality when working with algorithms that keep track of state.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=10)** In this lesson, we'll look at how to use the stack data structure to solve software problems.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=16)** Let's say we want to print the next greater element for every element in the array.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=23)** The next greater element for a given element is the first greater element on the right side of the array.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=30)** If no greater elements exist, then the next greater element is a sentinel value, negative one.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=37)** For example, if we were given an array with 5, 6, 3, and 50, the next greater element for 5 would be 6.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=46)** The next greater element for 6 would be 50 and the same for 3.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=52)** 50 does not have a next greater element to the right, so its value would be negative one.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=59)** In another example, let's say we have 15, 8, 4, and 10.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=65)** 15 does not have a next greater element, so it would be negative one.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=71)** Eight's next greater element to the right is 10 and the same for 4.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=76)** 15 is not to the right of 10 so it cannot be counted.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=81)** 10 is at the end of the array so it does not have a next greater element.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=86)** It's negative one.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=88)** Given this, there is a pattern to how we're working with the data.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=92)** We continue to look to the right of the array.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=96)** And if a given item does not have a match, we give it negative one.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=101)** We can use a stack to keep track of each element we've seen and see if the current array element is the next greater element.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=110)** In the algorithm, we'll push the first item in the array onto the stack.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=116)** Let's say it's from our last example, 15, then we'll iterate through the array and one by one, try to print out the next greater element for the item on top of the stack.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=129)** If the item we find is not greater, we'll push it on top of the stack.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=134)** In this case, 8 is not greater than 15 so we push 18 onto the stack.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=141)** The number on top of the stack will always be lesser than the ones below it.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=146)** This means any items that remain on the stack after the iteration will have the next greater element of negative one.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=155)** Now the stack contains 8 and 15 and the current is 4.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=160)** We'll continue with our algorithm for trying to find the next greater element for the item on top of the stack.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=167)** If we peak at 8 on the stack, 4 is not the next greater element.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=172)** 4 is less than 8 so we'll push 4 onto the stack.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=176)** Now the current is 10 and the stack contains 4, 8, and 15.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=182)** 10 is the next greater element of 4 so we'll pop it off the stack and print out the pair 4, 10.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=191)** 10 is also the next greater element of 8 so we'll print out 8, 10.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=197)** This is where the benefit of the stack comes in.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=200)** We didn't have to reiterate through the list in order to match 8 to 10.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=205)** We used the stack to keep track of the items we've already seen.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=209)** And as we iterate through the array, we match the stack items with their next greater element.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=216)** Continuing on, 10 is not greater than 15 so we'll push 10 onto the stock.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=223)** Now we've reached the end of the array and the stack contains 10 and 15.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=229)** Both of these do not have a next greater element so we'll pop each off and point their next greater element to negative one.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=237)** With that, we've printed each item of the array with their next greater element.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=244)** With this implementation, we iterate through the array trying to find the next greater element for the item on top of the stack.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=252)** If the array item we see is not the next greater element, we put it on top of the stack.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=259)** If the array item is the next greater element, then we continue to use the pop and peek operations to iterate through the stack.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=268)** We check if the array item matches as the next greater element for these stack elements.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=274)** If we run into a case where the stack is empty or the array item is not the next greater element, then we continue to iterate through the array.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=284)** When we've reached the end of the array, each item that's still in the stack must have a next greater element of negative one.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=293)** Using the stack data structure allowed us to iterate through the array only once rather than multiple times or even n times.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-theorizing-an-algorithm?u=76281980&t=301)** This gives us a linear time complexity.

#### Stack algorithms: Implementing next greater element
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's translate the next greater element algorithm into C# code.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=5)** The first step is to create the function.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=8)** We have printNextGreaterElement that takes in an integer array arr.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=14)** In order to print the next greater element for each item in the array, the array must have items.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=21)** If it does not have items, then there's nothing to print out.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=28)** We'll just write a new line and return.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=32)** If the array has items, we need to find those next greater elements.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=37)** We'll set up a stack to keep track of the elements we've already seen.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=42)** Since it's an integer array, the stack will also hold int.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=50)** We'll also push on that first element of the array.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=57)** Then we'll iterate through the array to find that next greater element.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=64)** In each iteration, we're checking whether or not a given item in the array is the next greatest element for the item on top of the stack.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=74)** However, this only works if the stack has items.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=78)** If the stack does not have items, then we simply push the next item onto the stack.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=84)** If the stack does have items, we'll pop off the top element and check if the current array element is its next greater match.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=96)** If the item we've popped off is less than next or that item in the array, then next is the next grader element for popped.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=106)** We'll write it out to the console.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=113)** Now this next item could be the next greater element for multiple items in the stack.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=119)** So let's change this if statement to a while loop.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=123)** While we continue to pop off items, if next is less than popped, then next is that next greater element and we'll write it out to the console.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=133)** To increment popped, we'll do stack.Pop.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=138)** Of course, we'll want to check that the stack has items before we try popping things off.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=144)** If the stack is empty, we'll want to break out of these loops.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=153)** Now after the while loop, the popped item is either equal to or greater than the next element.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=160)** If popped is greater than the next element, then we still need to find its next greater element, so we'll push it on top of the stack.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=171)** At this point in the implementation, we've printed out all the items that have a next greater element.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=178)** The current items in the stack are the items that do not have the next greater element.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=184)** We can display these by emptying the stack and printing out each element's next as negative one.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=191)** While the count is greater than zero, we'll pop off each item and display its next greatest at negative one.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=204)** In the main method, we have some test cases.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=207)** Let's run it.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=210)** And it works as expected.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=213)** Four's next greater element is 10.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=215)** Same with eight.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=217)** For 10, 15, those do not have a next greater element, so it's negative one.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=223)** For two, it's the only item in the array, so it's negative one.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=227)** Two and three, two has the next greater element of three and three does not have a next greater element so it's negative one.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=236)** For the last array, there are no items and so we get an empty line.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=241)** It's important to note that the assumption of the next greater element always being to the right played a significant role in the implementation.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=251)** With the stack, we were able to keep track of the items we still needed to find a next greater match for as we continue to iterate through the array.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=261)** If we got to the end of the array and still had some items where we did not find a match, we were able to retrieve those and give them the next greater element of negative one.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/stack-algorithms-implementing-next-greater-element?u=76281980&t=272)** Understanding how to leverage stacks in algorithms where you need to keep track of state is key to creating efficient solutions.

#### Solution: evaluate reverse polish notation

>

>

>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=52)** We can use these helper methods in our implementation in order to keep our code more concise.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=58)** Let's implement EvaluateRPN.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=63)** We know that each operand comes before its operator.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=68)** This means we need to keep track of each number until we see its operator.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=74)** We can do this with a stack.

>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=97)** Given this, we can split the string into tokens using the split method, with the space as a delimiter.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=107)** This will return an array containing only the operands and operators.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=113)** To run the calculations, we can iterate through this array, handling the operands and operators accordingly.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=122)** This is where our helper methods are going to come into play.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=128)** If the token is the number, we'll push it onto the stack, saving it until we see the operator, otherwise, if it's an operator,
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=152)** we'll pop the operands from the stack and perform the operation.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=160)** If there is only one operator and two operands in the expression, this would work and we could return the results.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=170)** However, there can be multiple operations within a single expression.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=176)** To account for this, we can push the intermediate result from the operation onto the stack.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=186)** Then when the next operator is found, the intermediate result is already on the stack and ready to be pulled for the operation.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=198)** Once we've iterated through the entire string, we can return the final result, which is sitting at the top of the stack.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=208)** That's our implementation.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=211)** Let's run it.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=215)** In our test code, we add three and four.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=221)** The result is seven.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=224)** Our solution works as expected.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=227)** To improve our code, we could add more error checking.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=231)** Currently, we assume the input is a valid RPN expression, but what if it wasn't?
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=238)** How could we account for that?
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=240)** We also know that a given operation can only have two operands or two inputs.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=247)** This means instead of a stack, we could have used variables to keep track of the intermediate data and operands.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=255)** This would take less space, but it would likely be harder to read and understand the code.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/challenge-evaluate-reverse-polish-notation-19868187?u=76281980&t=261)** It's important to understand the tradeoffs when deciding how to implement a solution.

### 5. Algorithms for Hash-Based Structures

#### Hash-based structures in C#
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Hash structures are great when working with collections, sets, and data formatted as key-value pairs.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=8)** In C-sharp, there are a few different options we can use.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=13)** First, there's the HashSet.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=15)** The HashSet is a collection of unique items.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=19)** It cannot contain duplicates, and unlike many of the tools we've used previously, the order of the items is not relevant.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=28)** It's great if you need to keep track of a unique list of items.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=32)** For example, let's say you're processing some purchases and you need to validate whether the vendor code is valid using a list of vendor codes.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=43)** You could store the vendor codes with the HashSet.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=46)** Then, quickly check if the code is valid in 01 time.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=51)** C-sharp also has a dictionary type.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=54)** This is especially useful if you need to work with key-value pairs.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=59)** Let's say you have a series of employees and you need to look up information about a given employee using their ID.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=68)** With a dictionary, you can store each employee value using the ID.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=74)** Then, you can quickly look up an employee by their ID in constant time.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=80)** If there's an identifier you can use to navigate your data, like an ID or a code, the dictionary type proves to be a very helpful tool in creating your algorithms.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=92)** There is also the Hashtable type.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=95)** This type is very similar to the dictionary type in that it stores key-value pairs, but there are a few differences in their implementations.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=105)** The dictionary type is a generic collection while the Hashtable structure is not.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=111)** With the Hashtable, there's no need to specify the type of key or value, but with the dictionary, those types are required.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=121)** If you try to access a key that isn't present, the dictionary will give an error, but the Hashtable will just return null.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=130)** The dictionary maintains the order of stored values while the Hashtable does not.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=136)** The Hashtable is also thread safe while the dictionary is only thread safe for public static members.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=144)** There are other differences between these types as well.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=148)** Ultimately, the dictionary is often recommended because it adds type safety, eliminating the unboxing and reboxing of your data.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/c-sharp-algorithms/hash-based-structures-in-c-sharp?u=76281980&t=158)** Understanding the tools available to you is essential so you can choose the right one for your algorithm.
